   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d18299668" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>A sulfur vacancy-rich, Sn-doped as well as carbon-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite (Vs-SMS@C) is rationally synthesized via a simple hydrothermal method combined with ball-milling reduction, which enhances the sodium storage performance. Benefiting from the 3D fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport network composed of the defective carbon coating, MoSC bonds, enlarged interlayer spacing, S-vacancies, and lattice distortion in the composite, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics is significantly accelerated. As expected, Vs-SMS@C releases an ultrahigh reversible capacity of 1089 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>, higher than the theoretical capacity. It delivers a satisfactory capacity of 463 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>1</sup>, which is the state-of-the-art rate capability compared to other MoS<sub>2</sub> based sodium ion battery anodes to the knowledge. Moreover, a super long-term cycle stability is achieved by Vs-SMS@C, which keeps 91.6% of the initial capacity after 3000 cycles under the current density of 5 A g<sup>1</sup> in the voltage of 0.33.0&nbsp;V. The sodium storage mechanism of Vs-SMS@C is investigated by employing electrochemical methods and ex situ techniques. The synergistic effect between S-vacancies and doped-Sn is evidenced by DFT calculations. This work opens new ideas for seeking excellent metal sulfide anodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309112-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309112-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The large-scale use of lithium-ion batteries and the scarcity of lithium resources on earth are difficult to reconcile so that people pay increasing attention to sodium-ion batteries. However, the larger radius of sodium ions tends to slow down the diffusion kinetics and cause volume expansion and degradation of electrode materials, which hinders the practical application of sodium-ion batteries (SIB).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0001_R_d18299660e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Molybdenum disulfide, as a typical layered transition metal sulfide (TMS), possesses large interlayer spacing (0.62&nbsp;nm) for sodium ion transmission and a high theoretical sodium storage capacity (670 mAh g<sup>1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0002_R_d18299660e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is considered as a promising anode material for SIB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0003_R_d18299660e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Appl. Surf. Sci. ACS Nano ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the poor electronic conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub> and the severe volume changes during the sodium insertion/extraction processes lead to inferior rate performance and short service life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0004_R_d18299660e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Energy Chem. J. Power Sources">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To resolve the problems, methods such as morphology regulation, inter-layer expansion, and composite with conductive substrates are commonly employed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0005_R_d18299660e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the diffusion velocity of ions in solid electrode is crucial to the charge-discharge rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0006_R_d18299660e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev. Chem. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Gap diffusion and vacancy diffusion are the two transmission forms of ions in crystal materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0007_R_d18299660e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, defect engineering (doping, vacancies, etc.) becomes an effective strategy for improving electrochemical performance of electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0002_R_d18299660e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0008_R_d18299660e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ACS Nano Electrochim. Acta Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Doping heteroatoms in electrode materials can tailor the internal electronic structure to achieve fast charge transport and provide more active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0009_R_d18299660e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Energy Appl. Surf. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Jin et&nbsp;al. synthesized a Co-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> with enlarged interlayer spacing and revealed a large number of active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0010_R_d18299660e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xie et&nbsp;al. prepared Fe doped GaN anode for lithium-ion battery through chemical vapor deposition, which significantly improved the rate performance and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0011_R_d18299660e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Arab. J. Chem.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theoretical calculations confirmed that the electronic structure of GaN was changed by Fe doping, thus raising the conductivity and ion transfer efficiency.</p>
         
         <p>Vacancy construction is another effective way for regulating the electronic structure of crystals and facilitating ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0012_R_d18299660e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Sci. China Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wang et&nbsp;al. introduced oxygen vacancies into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> lattice with the electrical conductivity improving, and theoretical calculations revealed that the density of states around the Fermi level increased and the Li-insertion energy barrier decreased in the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4-x</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0013_R_d18299660e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Sci. China Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Constructing sulfur vacancies in transition metal sulfides can also improve conductivity and generate active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0002_R_d18299660e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0008_R_d18299660e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ACS Nano Electrochim. Acta Adv. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0014_R_d18299660e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Appl. Catal. B: Environ.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Shao et&nbsp;al. knocked out some sulfur atoms of SnS<sub>2</sub> by annealing process to enhance the catalytic ability, and calculations confirmed that the S vacancies altered surface charge and enhanced electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0015_R_d18299660e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Sci. China Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the analysis, it is easy to find out that the sodium storage performance of MoS<sub>2</sub> and other TMS may be further improved by coupling of sulfur vacancies (Vs) and doping metals, while there are few relevant research reports.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a sulfur vacancy-rich, Sn-doped as well as carbon-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite (Vs-SMS@C) has been developed by hydrothermal synthesis method combined with ball-milling reduction treatment to boost the Na-storage performance (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0001">1a</a>). Under the coupling of Vs and doped Sn, a 3D fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport network forms in the composite, which is consist of defects in carbon layer, MoSC bonds at the interface of coating and substrate, expanded d-spacing, Vs passages, and lattice distortion, thereby accelerating the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer and reaction kinetics of the electrode. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations attest the synergistic effect between Vs and doped Sn that on enhancing the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> and reducing the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier of the composite. As a result, the Vs-SMS@C delivers an ultrahigh reversible capacity of 1089 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>. It also exhibits an outstanding rate capability and an excellent cycle stability, which releases a satisfactory capacity of 463 mAh g<sup>1</sup> under the high current density of 10 A g<sup>1</sup> with a low average attenuation of 0.08% per cycle in 600 cycles. Surprisingly, controlling the charge-discharge voltage as 0.3-3.0&nbsp;V, Vs-SMS@C displays a super-long cycle reversibility, which acquires a high-capacity retention of 91.6% even after 3000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>1</sup>. Furthermore, the strategy of coupling Vs and doping metals can be further used for precise design and process of other high-performance TMS anodes toward practical SIB.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202309112-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/73491d35-030d-4a99-86c9-413513ea8e46/smll202309112-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/73491d35-030d-4a99-86c9-413513ea8e46/smll202309112-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/48605543-39fd-4157-bd76-d51e812359fd/smll202309112-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/73491d35-030d-4a99-86c9-413513ea8e46/smll202309112-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309112-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309112" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The preparation process, b,c) SEM photographs, and d) element mappings of Vs-SMS@C; TEM photographs of e) MS@C, f) SMS@C, and g) Vs-SMS@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309112-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309112-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The SEM photographs reveal that there are 3D microsphere flower with a diameter of 500&nbsp;nm for SM@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and SMS@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), which is assembled from ultra-thin (20&nbsp;nm) nanosheets. By ball milling reduction treatment, the nanosheets in Vs-SMS@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0001">1b,c</a>) are distorted and peeled off, and their thickness reduces to 10&nbsp;nm. The element mappings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information) indicate that C, Mo, S, and Sn elements are uniformly distributed in the composites. The high-resolution TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0001">1eg</a>) show that the d-spacing of (002) plane in MS@C, SMS@C and Vs-SMS@C is 0.627, 0.646, and 0.703&nbsp;nm, respectively, which indicates that the interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> can be expanded by Sn-doping and further enlarged due to the introduction of Vs. The lattice fringes in Vs-SMS@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0001">1g</a>) become very chaotic, further revealing that the ball milling process causes considerable lattice distortion.</p>
         
         <p>XRD analysis (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2a</a>) demonstrates that the diffraction peaks of the three composites match well to the standard card of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (PDF#37-1492). Since MoS<sub>2</sub> lamellas are stacked along the (002) crystal plane, the number of stacking layers in crystal of the composites (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) can be calculated semi-quantitatively by Scherrer formula.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0016_R_d18299660e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Appl. Catal. B: Environ.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The layer number decreases from 9 in MS@C to 7 in SMS@C and 6 in Vs-SMS@C gradually, indicating that both Sn-doping and Vs can reduce the stacking layers of MoS<sub>2</sub>. The fewer the layers of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, the larger the d-spacing, which is consistent with TEM results. The structural information of the composites is further investigated by Raman spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The Raman shifts at 370, and 400 cm<sup>1</sup> are indexed to E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> (In-Plane MoS bond) and A<sub>1g</sub> (Out-of-Plane Mo-S bond) modes, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0017_R_d18299660e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks of E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> in Vs-SMS@C move to lower wavenumber than those of SMS@C, implying the increase of Vs in Vs-SMS@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0018_R_d18299660e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The interval between E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes of Vs-SMS@C is significantly narrower than that of SMS@C and further narrower than that of MS@C, corresponding to the enlarged d-spacing caused by Sn doping and Vs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0004_R_d18299660e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Energy Chem. J. Power Sources">4</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0019_R_d18299660e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Appl. Catal. B: Environ.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The significant increase in d-spacing of Vs-SMS@C is the results of weakened van der Waals interactions in interlayers due to the absence of S and the few-layer structure induced by the thin nanosheets of MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0020_R_d18299660e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloys Compd.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The carbon content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) of MS@C, SMS@C, and Vs-SMS@C is quantitatively determined as 4.3, 3.4, and 10.3 wt.% by TG analyzer, respectively. The remarkably higher carbon content in Vs-SMS@C is attributed to the carbonization of some ascorbic acid during the ball milling reduction.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202309112-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d024f31-5c34-43dd-b58d-bd7bb916686f/smll202309112-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d024f31-5c34-43dd-b58d-bd7bb916686f/smll202309112-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/54d968eb-a3eb-4b11-9871-cfe2c465bb71/smll202309112-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d024f31-5c34-43dd-b58d-bd7bb916686f/smll202309112-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309112-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309112" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) Raman diagrams, c) EPR results, and df) XPS spectra of the composites.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>EPR test is conducted to evaluate the Vs concentration of the composites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2d</a>). In MS@C and SMS@C, weak resonance signals are observed near g = 2.004, indicating the presence of a small amount of Vs in both composites. Notably, Vs-SMS@C shows a significantly stronger EPR signal at the same g-value, indicating the generation of extensive Vs during the ball milling reduction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0021_R_d18299660e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Vs concentrations of the composites are further analyzed by XPS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2df</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). According to the semi-quantitative analysis of XPS plots, the atomic ratios of S/Mo in MS@C and SMS@C are 1.74 and 1.73, respectively, corresponding to Vs concentrations of 13% and 13.5%, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Remarkably, the S/Mo atomic ratio of Vs-SMS@C reduces to 1.29, and the Vs concentration correspondingly increases to 35.5%. The elemental content of Vs-SMS@C has been further analyzed with ICP-OES, and the results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). Accordingly, the Vs concentration of Vs-SMS@C is calculated to be 34.8%, which is basically consistent with the XPS results.</p>
         
         <p>The C 1s spectrum of the composites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information) can be fitted as CS (284.3&nbsp;eV), CC (284.8&nbsp;eV), CN (285.5&nbsp;eV), CO (286.5&nbsp;eV) and OCO (298&nbsp;eV) bonds, demonstrating S and N doping into the carbon coating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0022_R_d18299660e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> S-N doping is beneficial for improving the electronic conductivity of the carbon layer and inducing the generation of carbon vacancies. Finally, it facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0023_R_d18299660e807" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci. Rare Met. Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of CS bonds is conducive to the formation of CSMo interfacial bonds between the carbon coating and the MoS<sub>2</sub> substrate, serving as sodium storage channels to promote sodium ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0024_R_d18299660e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The S 2s spectrum of the composites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2e</a>) can be fitted as two peaks, relating to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (162.43&nbsp;eV) and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (163.64&nbsp;eV) orbits of Mo-S bonds, respectively. While the S 2p spectrum of Vs-SMS@C exhibits a weak peak at 170.22&nbsp;eV that belongs to S-O bond, implying that the MoS<sub>2</sub> surface is partially oxidized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0022_R_d18299660e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is further confirmed by the significantly enhanced intensity of the O 1s spectral in Vs-SMS@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to MS@C, the characteristic peaks of Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> in SMS@C shift slightly to lower binding energies, suggesting that the electron density of MoS<sub>2</sub> can be increased by Sn doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2d,e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0018_R_d18299660e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0020_R_d18299660e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloys Compd.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the binding energies of Mo 3d, S 2p, and Sn 3d in Vs-SMS@C are higher than those in SMS@C, which may be attributed to the partial oxidation and massive lattice distortion of Vs-SMS@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0002">2df</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0015_R_d18299660e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Sci. China Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Some S atomics are substituted by O to form strong Mo-O bonds, which is beneficial to strengthen the structural stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0025_R_d18299660e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of the composites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information) conform to the classic Type IV curve, implying the mesoporous characteristics. The detailed pore size distributions of Vs-SMS@C(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information) manifest the richer mesopores than other two composites. Evidently, Vs-SMS@C obtains a larger specific surface area (23.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup>) than SMS@C (17.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup>) and MS@C (13.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup>), resulting in a larger reaction contact area in electrode, thus improving the rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0026_R_d18299660e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage properties of the composites are studied in button cells with sodium discs as counter electrodes. The first cathodic process in the CV plots (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3a</a>) shows three reduction peaks near 2.0, 0.7, and 0.5&nbsp;V, which are attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> to form Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>, the generation of solid electrolyte film (SEI) and the formation of Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0027_R_d18299660e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the low carbon content of the composites, the cathodic peak of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the carbon layer may be covered by the peak at 0.5 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0001_R_d18299660e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the initial anodic scan of CV curves, an oxidation peak associated with the oxidation of Mo to Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> and the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> is detected at 1.9 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0027_R_d18299660e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The CV curves of the subsequent cycles overlap well, implying the excellent structural stability and electrochemical reversibility of Vs-SMS@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202309112-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/335ced19-0b33-4a1f-ab50-c228281caa4c/smll202309112-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/335ced19-0b33-4a1f-ab50-c228281caa4c/smll202309112-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/447f5967-00ad-459f-83ce-431949dfe306/smll202309112-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/335ced19-0b33-4a1f-ab50-c228281caa4c/smll202309112-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309112-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309112" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of the composites. a) First CV curves at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>1</sup>, b) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of Vs-SMS@C at 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>, c) rate performances, d) comparison of sodium storage performance with other MoS<sub>2</sub> composites in literatures, e) dQ/dV curves of Vs-SMS@C from 6th to 200th cycle at 5 A g<sup>1</sup>, f) cycling performances at 5 A g<sup>1</sup>, g) long-cycle performance of the composites at 10 A g<sup>1</sup>, h) Full battery schematic diagram of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C//Vs-SMS@C, i) charge/discharge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup> of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C cathode, Vs-SMS@C anode, and the full cell, j) rate performance of the full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of Vs-SMS@C are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3b</a>. The electrode delivers an enormous initial specific capacity (2163 mAh g<sup>1</sup>) and retains an amazing reversible capacity of 1089 mAh g<sup>1</sup>. While the initial specific capacities of SMS@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information) and MS@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information) decrease to 1493 and 926 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, respectively, and the reversible capacities retain just 596 and 372 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, separately. The irreversible capacity of the first cycle is mainly generated by the decomposition of electrolytes to form SEI film, which generally rises along with the BET area of electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0028_R_d18299660e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial specific capacity and reversible capacity increase gradually in MS@C, SMS@C, and Vs-SMS@C, moreover, the reversible capacity obtained in Vs-SMS@C exceed the theoretical value (670 mAh g<sup>1</sup>), suggesting that the doped Sn and Vs play crucial roles in producing active sites for Na storage. Besides, the initial coulomb efficiency (47.2%) of Vs-SMS@C is greater than those of SMS@C (39.8%) and MS@C (39.8%), illustrating that Vs is beneficial to enhancing the reversibility of MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>The rate performances of the three materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3c</a>. The Sn-doped material (SMS@C) exhibits an improved rate capability compared to MS@C. It delivers reversible capacities of 596 and 312 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.1 and 10 A g<sup>1</sup>, respectively, and then returns to 600mAh g<sup>1</sup> at current density of 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>. Due to the introduction of Vs, the rate performance of Vs-SMS@C is greatly enhanced. When the current densities are 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>1</sup>, the average discharge capacities of Vs-SMS@C are 1286, 961, 885, 800, 683, and 567 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. Even at an ultrahigh current density of 10 A g<sup>1</sup>, a satisfactory reversible capacity of 463 mAh g<sup>1</sup> is obtained. As the current density returns to 0.1A g<sup>1</sup>, a high reversible capacity is restored as 1064 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, demonstrating the excellent structural durability of Vs-SMS@C. The optimized rate performance of SMS@C and Vs-SMS@C is related to the enhanced electronic and ionic conductivity by coupling of Sn doping and Vs. To our surprise, compared to other MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes reported so far for SIBs, Vs-SMS@C obtains an evidently superior rate capability, indicating its promising development prospects (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0001_R_d18299660e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano J. Mater. Chem. A">1</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0028_R_d18299660e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0029_R_d18299660e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Sci. Chem. Eng. J. Small Methods Small Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Galvanostatic charge/discharge process of the composites at 1 A g<sup>1</sup> for five cycles is conducted for electrochemical activation, and then cyclic performance is performed at a current density of 5 A g<sup>1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3f</a>). Vs-SMS@C delivers a high reversible capacity of 688 mAh g<sup>1</sup> in the first cycle of 5 A g<sup>1</sup> and obtains 429 mAh g<sup>1</sup> even after 200 cycles with a retention of 62.4%. In contrary, the initial discharge capacities of SMS@C and MS@C at 5 A g<sup>1</sup> are 347and 245 mAh g<sup>1</sup>, respectively, and quickly decrease to 140 and 118 mAh g<sup>1</sup> after 200 cycles, separately.</p>
         
         <p>The plots of differential capacity versus voltage (dQ/dV) in different cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) are employed to explain the reason of the rapid capacity decay in SMS@C and MS@C. The oxidation and reduction peaks of Vs-SMS@C remain clear throughout the first 200 cycles, while the peaks of SMS@C and MS@C decay gradually with the cycles increasing, demonstrating that Vs promotes the reversibility of Na storage.</p>
         
         <p>The fast charge/discharge ability and long-cycle stability of Vs-SMS@C are evaluated at an ultrahigh current density of 10 A g<sup>1</sup>(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3g</a>). After charge-discharge five cycles at 1 A g<sup>1</sup> for activation, Vs-SMS@C delivers an ideal capacity of 408 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>1</sup>, and remains as 210 mAh g<sup>1</sup> after 600 cycles, with an attenuation of only 0.08% per cycle in average. In contrast, the reversible capacities of SMS@C and MS@C are just 93.9 and 59.7 mAh g<sup>1</sup> after 600 cycles at 10 A g<sup>1</sup>, with decay rates of 0.11% and 0.13% per cycle, respectively. The excellent fast charge/discharge capability of Vs-SMS@C is attributed to the improved electron conductivity and the 3D Na<sup>+</sup> fast transmission network that composed of defective carbon layer, C-S-Mo interfacial bonds, enlarged d-spacing, Vs and lattice distortion. Meanwhile, the volume expansion caused by Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation in Vs-SMS@C can be also alleviated by the carbon coating, expanded interlayer spacing and Vs, prompting the excellent long-cycle stability. The increased polarity of the carbon layer due to S-N doping and the electronic localized centers formed by coupling of Vs and Sn can reinforce the binding of polysulfides generated in the conversion reaction, further extending the cycle life.</p>
         
         <p>Considering the irreversibility of conversion reaction in MoS<sub>2</sub> during low voltage discharge, the feasibility of extending electrode life by controlling voltage has been investigated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0030_R_d18299660e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochim. Acta">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Excitingly, although the reversible capacity reduces to 225 mAh g<sup>1</sup> when the test voltage is confined to 0.33.0&nbsp;V with the current of 5 A g<sup>1</sup>, an ultrahigh capacity retention of 91.6% is achieved after cycling for 3000 loops (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information). The redox peaks in the corresponding dV/dQ curves highly overlap in the first 1000 cycles, demonstrating that increasing the discharge cutoff voltage can effectively avoid irreversible reactions and significantly prolong the service life of Vs-SMS@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Full cells are assembled to further assess the practical application behavior of Vs-SMS@C, in which Vs-SMS@C is used as anode and a self-made carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP@C) is employed as the cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3h</a>). The relevant structure and performance of the NVP@C is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). Before assembling the full cell, the Vs-SMS@C anode is charge-discharged for two cycles as presodiation to remove the side reaction with electrolyte. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of NVP@C cathode, Vs-SMS@C anode, and the full cell are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0003">3i</a>. The voltage ranges of NVP@C and the full cell operated in the tests are 1.74.0&nbsp;V and 0.53.7&nbsp;V, separately. The full cell releases a reversible capacity of 138 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>. Even at a high current density of 2 A g<sup>1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 35 mAh g<sup>1</sup> is remained. Furthermore, the reversible capacity recovers to 120mAh g<sup>1</sup> when the current density is reset to 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>, showing excellent rate performance and cycle stability.</p>
         
         <p>CV curves vasus scanning rates are investigated to reveal the charge storage mechanism of the composites (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202309112-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information), and the detailed calculations are exhibited in Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The <i>b</i>-values computed from the oxidation/reduction peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) of MS@C, SMS@C, and Vs-SMS@C are 0.90/0.94, 1.00/0.99 and 0.99/0.95, respectively, indicating that the pseudocapacitive behavior dominants the Na storage mechanism in the composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0031_R_d18299660e1211" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacitance contributions at different scanning rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0004">4b,c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a><a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) are further calculated by Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), which increases as the scan rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0032_R_d18299660e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacitance contributions at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>1</sup> of MS@C, SMS@C, and Vs-SMS@C are 82%, 85%, and 92%, separately, indicating the pseudocapacitive sodium storage mechanism of the composites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0028_R_d18299660e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, at the same scanning rate, the capacitance contribution of the composite increases after Sn-doping, and further increases after introducing Vs, suggesting the faster reaction kinetic and better structural durability of Vs-SMS@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0033_R_d18299660e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high pseudo capacitance contribution of Vs-SMS@C stems from the abundant Na storage active sites, 3D fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport network that endowed by coupling of Sn and Vs, as well as the shortened Na<sup>+</sup> migration distance due to the few-layer structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0034_R_d18299660e1252" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202309112-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1dc1369b-38dd-470a-af2f-2ff88c24712d/smll202309112-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1dc1369b-38dd-470a-af2f-2ff88c24712d/smll202309112-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/15fe6996-ab28-45bb-bf6a-992928528507/smll202309112-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1dc1369b-38dd-470a-af2f-2ff88c24712d/smll202309112-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309112-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309112" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at different scanning rates, b) the capacitance contribution (red region) in the CV curve at 0.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>1</sup>, and d) GITT curves of Vs-SMS@C; c) capacitance contributions versus scanning rates, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient versus potential in e) discharge and f) charge process of the composites.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>GITT measurement is performed to further demonstrate the Na storage kinetics of the composites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0004">4df</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). All composites display the similar GITT profiles, suggesting that their sodium storage mechanism are the same.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0035_R_d18299660e1290" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, the Vs-SMS@C shows higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) than other two composites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0004">4e,f</a>), revealing that the 3D Na transport network in Vs-SMS@C is very effective in facilitating the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer.</p>
         
         <p>EIS test (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) is implemented on the composites and the relevant impedance values (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) are fitted according to the equivalent circuit (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). The charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of Vs-SMS@C (221.8 ) is smaller than that of SMS@C (267.7 ) and further smaller than that of MS@C (503.5 ), illustrating that the charge migration between the electrode surface and electrolyte can be accelerated by the coupling of Vs and Sn. Importantly, the cycled Vs-SMS@C exhibits a super low <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 16.6 , which may be attributed to the generation of highly conductive 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> verified in subsequent ex situ characterizations (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0036_R_d18299660e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater. Nano Energy">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202309112-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b7c7a558-fa59-46d0-9fa9-a1e4516a97a0/smll202309112-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b7c7a558-fa59-46d0-9fa9-a1e4516a97a0/smll202309112-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1c086ed2-b1a6-4793-930a-0fd52913b601/smll202309112-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b7c7a558-fa59-46d0-9fa9-a1e4516a97a0/smll202309112-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309112-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309112" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ characterizations of Vs-SMS@C at different states in first cycle, a) Ex situ Raman spectra, b) ex situ XRD patterns, c) XPS curves, and d) ex situ HRTEM photographs (The letters C and D in the figure represent charge and discharge, respectively).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Warburg impedance (W<sub>1</sub>) in the low-frequency of EIS plot reflects the diffusion ability of Na<sup>+</sup>. The impedance factors () are fitted according to the plots of Z versus <sup>1/2</sup> in low-frequency, then <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> for different electrodes are calculated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of MS@C (2.76  10<sup>13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup>) suddenly raises to 8.56  10<sup>13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup> after Sn-doping, and then further increases to 9.37  10<sup>13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>1</sup> after the introduction of Vs, reflecting the same variation trend as the GITT results. It is confirmed again that the excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of Vs-SMS@C is significantly endowed by the coupling of Vs and Sn.</p>
         
         <p>In order to deeply explore the Na storage mechanism, the structural evolution of Vs-SMS@C electrode in the first cycle is investigated by ex situ characterizations. The ex situ Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5a</a>) show that the intensity of E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> peak weakens as the discharge proceeding. When Vs-SMS@C is discharged to the cutoff voltage (0.005&nbsp;V), the E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> peaks completely vanish, corresponding to the fracture of MoS bonds and the generation of Na<sub>2</sub>S. Interestingly, the E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> peaks appear again after Vs-SMS@C is charged to 3.0&nbsp;V, suggesting the formation of MoS bonds and the excellent chemical reversibility of Vs-SMS@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0037_R_d18299660e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the A<sub>1g</sub> peak shifts slightly toward higher wave numbers in the discharge process, demonstrating the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption storage mechanism, which contributes to the pseudocapacitive behavior of Vs-SMS@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0033_R_d18299660e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#smll202309112-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0038_R_d18299660e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5b</a> shows the ex situ XRD patterns of Vs-SMS@C. As the discharge going on, the diffraction peak at 14.2 corresponding to crystal plane (002) of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> reduces gradually and migrates toward lower angle, demonstrating that the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion leads to the enlargement of interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0029_R_d18299660e1441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Sci. Chem. Eng. J. Small Methods Small Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As Vs-SMS@C is fully discharged to 0.005&nbsp;V, the diffraction signals of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> disappear and the characteristic peaks belonged to Na<sub>2</sub>S (12.7) and Mo (56.0) emerge, implying that the discharge reaction of MoS<sub>2</sub> is fully completed. In the subsequent charge process, the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo gradually disappear, while the characteristic peak of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> at 33.0 appears once again, implying the excellent electrochemical reversibility of Vs-SMS@C. Notably, the generation of 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> is confirmed, as a weak peak is observed at 6.8 in XRD patterns of the charged samples, which is in favor of boosting the conductivity of the electrode and reducing the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier of Vs-SMS@C.</p>
         
         <p>The ex situ XPS curves of Vs-SMS@C electrodes are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5c</a>. The binding energy of Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> in Vs-SMS@C increases after discharged to 0.005&nbsp;V, which is related to the expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> caused by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, leading to a weakened interlayer interaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20d</a>, Supporting Information). Interestingly, as the electrode is charged back to 3.0&nbsp;V, the binding energy of Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> drops lower than that of the initial electrode, which is ascribed to the generation of some 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> during the charging process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5c</a>). The binding energy of S 2p declines in the discharge electrode and further reduces in the charged sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20c</a>, Supporting Information). The decrease in binding energy of S 2p during discharge attributes to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, increasing the electron concentration around S atoms, while the decrease after charging owes to the presence of 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0039_R_d18299660e1500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nat. Chem.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is consistent with the analysis results of ex situ XRD.</p>
         
         <p>The phase and structural evolution of Vs-SMS@C during cycling is thoroughly revealed by ex situ HRTEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5d</a>). Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo produced by the conversion reaction are observed in the discharged electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5d-II,III</a>), while 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> are detected in the sample when charged back to 3.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5d-IV,V</a>), demonstrating the excellent cyclic reversibility of Vs-SMS@C. The generation of 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> is caused by the obstruction of de-intercalation process of some Na<sup>+</sup>, which are strongly bound by the doped O. This is confirmed by the high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barriers of the O-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> that calculated by DFT (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). The 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> displays metallic properties with an ultra-high conductivity and a larger interlayer spacing (1.26&nbsp;nm, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0005">5d-VI</a>), which is beneficial to boosting the reaction kinetics of electrode.</p>
         
         <p>The first principles calculations are conducted to further clarify the inherent mechanism of outstanding sodium storage performance of Vs-SMS@C (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0006">6</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a><a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energies in different MoS<sub>2</sub> materials are displayed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). Compared to pure MoS<sub>2</sub>, the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy of each site increases after Sn doping, with the maximum value ascending from 1.78&nbsp;eV at the Mo top to 2.26&nbsp;eV at the Sn top, indicating that the Sn-doping can enhance the sodium storage capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Interestingly, although the introduction of Vs slightly reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energies of MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c</a>, Supporting Information), under the coupling Vs and Sn, the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energies rise to a higher level (3.02&nbsp;eV) than the Sn doped sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0006">6a</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), illustrating that there is a synergistic effect between Vs and doped Sn, thus promoting the sodium storage capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202309112-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8762ea7a-aad5-4958-b7f3-df121dd6fde4/smll202309112-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8762ea7a-aad5-4958-b7f3-df121dd6fde4/smll202309112-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff79fed0-190e-4037-9750-b759968d8dad/smll202309112-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8762ea7a-aad5-4958-b7f3-df121dd6fde4/smll202309112-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202309112-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202309112" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Atomic structures and calculation results of different MoS<sub>2</sub> materials. Stable adsorption sites of Na in a) Vs-Sn coupled MoS<sub>2</sub>, b) Sn-O co-doped MoS<sub>2</sub>; c) the schematic diagram of the optimal Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path in Vs-Sn coupled MoS<sub>2</sub> and related Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers; d) the schematic diagram of 3D fast sodium transmission network in of Vs-SMS@C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Considering the substitution of some S atoms by O, the influence of oxygen doping on the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy of MoS<sub>2</sub> is investigated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0006">6b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21d</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The O-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> depicts a lower Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy (1.41&nbsp;eV) than the pure MoS<sub>2</sub>, while the Sn-O co-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> displays a significantly increased Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy (2.92&nbsp;eV) than the Sn-doped sample, indicating that there is also a synergistic effect between Sn and O in MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers on surfaces of different MoS<sub>2</sub> materials are calculated to investigate the effect of Sn-Vs coupling on Na<sup>+</sup> movement. The CI-NEB method is used for calculating, and the minimum energy path is chosen as the diffusion path. The Na<sup>+</sup> moves from the hollow of hexagon to the adjacent hollow along the zigzag path on the MoS<sub>2</sub> layer surface, and the diffusion energy barriers are low (0.08 and 0.11&nbsp;eV), of which the Na<sup>+</sup> can migrate smoothly on the MoS<sub>2</sub> surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a</a>, Supporting Information). After Sn-doping, the diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> reduce to 0.05 and 0.11&nbsp;eV along the similar path, proving that Sn-doping is beneficial to the Na<sup>+</sup> transport (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22b</a>, Supporting Information). Although introduction of Vs increases the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers (0.17 and 0.49&nbsp;eV), the Vs-Sn coupled MoS<sub>2</sub> still demonstrates moderate values (0.15&nbsp;eV and 0.3&nbsp;eV), which meet the charge-discharge requirements of SIB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0006">6c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22c</a>, Supporting Information). The diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> in O-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.08 and 0.48&nbsp;eV) and Sn-O co-doped MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.07 and 0.50&nbsp;eV) are too high to facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22d,e</a>, Supporting Information). The doped O atomics are too few to fundamentally hinder the sodium ion movement. Instead, the O atomics promote the generation of 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>, thereby enhancing the conductivity of the electrode. Notably, the nanosheet morphology and rich lattice distortion of Vs-SMS@C greatly facilitate the acceleration Na<sup>+</sup> migration.</p>
         
         <p>The diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> through the Vs channels in MoS<sub>2</sub> are calculated to assess the possibility of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through MoS<sub>2</sub> layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0006">6e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). Mono sulfur vacancy channel (mono-Vs channel) is defined as below, the MoS<sub>2</sub> layer loses one S atom above and one S atom below the same position. The two-sulfur vacancy channel (two-Vs channel) is attributed to the MoS<sub>2</sub> layer which loses two S atoms above and two S atoms below the same position. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier of mono-Vs channel is 3.0&nbsp;eV, which is too high to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> transport. While the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier in two-Vs channel drops to 2.47&nbsp;eV, demonstrating that enlarging the Vs channel can reduce the resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> during the layer transport. As we know, the vacancies prefer to generate near doped atoms, demonstrating that the multi-Vs channels can be formed near Sn atoms of Vs-SMS@C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0040_R_d18299660e1743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Phys. Rev. B">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the high Vs concentration, it can be inferred that there are many multi-Vs channels in Vs-SMS@C for Na<sup>+</sup> transport.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above analyses, the outstanding sodium storage behavior of Vs-SMS@C is primarily attributed to the following structural merits (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202309112-fig-0004">4g</a>). First, the S/N co-doped carbon coating and the increased electron density contributed by Vs-Sn coupling enhance the surface and internal electronic conductivity of the material, separately. Second, the 3D Na<sup>+</sup> fast transport network which consists of the defective carbon layer, CSMo bonds acting as Na<sup>+</sup> storage channels, extended interlayer spacing, rich Vs, and abundant lattice distortion, significantly improves the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, enhancing the quickly charging and discharging ability. Thirdly, the ultra-thin nanosheet structure of Vs-SMS@C enlarges the reaction contact area and shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion distance in solid-state, further improving the rate performance. Most importantly, the synergistic effect of Vs and Sn raises the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy and cooperates with the extensive lattice distortion to develop generous active sites for sodium storage, thus resulting in an ultra-high reversible capacity. Finally, the volume variation during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process is together restricted by the carbon coating, expanded interlayer spacing, and Vs, hence strengthening the structural durability of the composite for prolonging cycle life.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309112-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309112-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, in order to promote the rate performance and cycling life, a rich Vs, Sn-doped, as well as carbon-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite, has been synthesized by a simple hydrothermal method and ball milling reduction treatment. Benefited from the 3D Na<sup>+</sup> fast transport network which consists of the defective carbon layer on the surface, CSMo bonds acting as Na<sup>+</sup> storage channels at the interface between the coating and MoS<sub>2</sub> matrix, extended interlayer spacing, rich Vs and massive lattice distortion, Vs-SMS@C exhibits an outstanding sodium storage performance. It delivered an exceptional high reversible capacity of 1089 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>1</sup>, and released a satisfactory capacity of 463 mAh g<sup>1</sup> at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>1</sup>. When controlling the operation voltage to 0.3-3.0&nbsp;V, an ultrahigh capacity retention of 91.6% is achieved by Vs-SMS@C after 3000 cycles with a current density of 5 A g<sup>1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>Dynamics researches reveal that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and the charge transfer rate are elevated by the coupling of Vs-Sn, thus facilitating the pseudocapacitive behavior. The ex situ measurements manifest that the volumetric expansion during the cycling process is restricted by the carbon coating, expanded interlayer spacing and Vs, which strengthens the structural durability and endows the excellent cyclic reversibility of the electrode. Some O atoms doped in Vs-SMS@C can strongly capture Na<sup>+</sup>, boosting the generation of 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>, further enhancing the electronic and ion conductivity. DFT calculations indicate that there is a synergistic effect between Vs and Sn to enhance the absorption of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby increasing the active sites for sodium storage. This work not only provides an excellent MoS<sub>2</sub> anode for SIB, but also is anticipated to provide a new way of coupling Vs and doping metal for developing high-performance metal sulfide anodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202309112-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202309112-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309112-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309112-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309112-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309112-sec-0070-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>Added 6.179&nbsp;g (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>6</sub>Mo<sub>7</sub>O<sub>24</sub>4H<sub>2</sub>O, 10.657&nbsp;g thiourea, 2.154&nbsp;g glucose monohydrate, and 0.124&nbsp;g SnCl<sub>4</sub>5H<sub>2</sub>O into 80&nbsp;mL deionized water, and stirred magnetically at room temperature for 30 min. Then, 1.261&nbsp;g oxalic acid dihydrate was added to deionized water of 150&nbsp;mL, and continued to stir until completely dissolved. The solution was transferred into a 200&nbsp;mL hydrothermal reactor and kept it at 220 C for 12 h, cooled down with the furnace.</p>
                  
                  <p>The reaction mixture was suction filtered, and the solid residue was alternately washed with deionized water and anhydrous ethanol for three times, respectively. After vacuum dried at 60 C for 8 h, the product was calcined in a tube furnace. The heat treatment process involved holding at 300 C for 20 min under argon protection and then heating to 900 C to keep for 3 h. After cooling and grinding, Sn-doped and carbon-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite (denoted as SMS@C) was obtained. For comparison, carbon-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite (MS@C) was prepared under the same conditions, excepting that no SnCl<sub>4</sub>5H<sub>2</sub>O was used.</p>
                  
                  <p>Weighed SMS@C and ascorbic acid in a molar ratio of 4:1, then mixed well and ball milled at a speed of 1000 r min<sup>1</sup> for 30 min to introduce sulfur vacancies. Separated the material from the grinding ball, washed with deionized water several times, then froze for 12&nbsp;h and vacuum freeze-dried for 24&nbsp;h. Took out the product and ground it into powder to obtain sulfur vacancy rich, Sn doped as well as carbon-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> composite (Vs-SMS@C).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309112-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309112-sec-0080-title"> Material Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The phase structures of the materials were studied by X-ray powder diffractometer (XRD, Rigaku Smartlab, Japan). The microstructures of the materials were observed by scanning electron microscope (SEM, ZEISS Sigma 300) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, FEI Tecnai G2F20), and the element compositions were determined by the attached energy dispersion spectrometer (EDS, Smartedx). Thermogravimetric analyzer (TG, TGAQ50) was employed to quantify the carbon content of the composites with a temperature range of 25700 C and a heating rate of 10 C min<sup>1</sup> under air atmosphere. Nitrogen adsorption/desorption tests (Micromeritics ASAP 2460) were conducted to analyze the specific surface areas and pore size distributions of the composites. Ramon spectrometer (Horiba LabRAM HR Evolution) with a laser wavelength of 532&nbsp;nm was used to investigate the structural information of the materials. The chemical states of elements in composites were surveyed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Scientific K-Alpha). Electron paramagnetic resonance spectrometer (EPR, Brukerb EMXplus-6/1) was utilized to analyze the concentration of sulfur vacancies in composites at room temperature. The elemental content of the samples was determined by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES: Agilent 5800).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309112-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309112-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The as-prepared sample (MS@C, SMS@C or Vs-SMS@C), binder (polyvinylidene fluoride, PVDF), and conductive agent (Ketjen Black) were weighed as a mass ratio of 7:1:2, and an appropriate amount of N-Methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) was added to grind into a slurry in an agate mortar. Coated the slurry evenly onto a copper foil with a coating machine, dried it in a vacuum drying oven at 60 C, and then cut it into discs with a diameter of 14&nbsp;mm as electrodes. Glass fiber and sodium disc were used as separator and counter electrode, respectively, and the solution of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC)/Dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1, v/v) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was used as electrolyte to assemble CR2016 button cell in an argon-filled glovebox. Charge and discharge tests with a voltage range of 0.0053.0&nbsp;V were conducted on the battery testing system (Shenzhen, Neware). The cyclic voltammetry curves (CV, 0.0053.0&nbsp;V) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS, 0.1100k Hz) were performed on an electrochemical workstation (CHI660E, Shanghai Chenhua). The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) plots were measured on a battery tester (Wuhan, Land), and the following steps were repeated for electrodes to the cut-off voltage: discharged or charged at a constant current for 10 min, then relaxed for 30 min until equilibrium.</p>
                  
                  <p>Weighed carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP@C), PVDF, and Koqin black according to the mass ratio of 7:1:2 to prepare cathodes, and then assembled sodium ion full batteries with the sodium pre-embedded Vs-SMS@C as anodes. The mass ratio of active substances of cathode/anode in the full battery was 3:1, and the specific capacity of the full batteries was calculated based on the mass of Vs-SMS@C.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202309112-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202309112-sec-0100-title"> Theoretical Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed in the Vienna ab initio Simulation Package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0041_R_d18299660e1905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Comp. Mater. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The PerdewBurkeErnzerhof functional of the Generalized Gradient Approximation (GGA-PBE) was used to describe the exchange-correlation energy, while the Projection Enhanced Wave (PAW) pseudopotential was chosen to describe the electron-ion interaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0042_R_d18299660e1910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cutoff energy was set as 320&nbsp;eV to expand the electron wave function. To minimize the impact of periodic boundaries, a vacuum distance of 20  was used to ensure a large vacuum environment. A 4  4  1 supercell was used to realize the migration of ions in a large area. To explore the migration properties of sodium on MoS<sub>2</sub> surface, the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method was used to calculate the migration barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202309112-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202309112-bib-0043_R_d18299660e1918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Chem. Phys.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
